<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Water</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about general aspects of water. For a detailed discussion of its physical and chemical properties, see <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">Properties of water</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Water_(disambiguation)" title="Water (disambiguation)">Water (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iceberg_with_hole_near_Sandersons_Hope_2007-07-28_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1176" data-file-width="2048" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Iceberg_with_hole_near_Sandersons_Hope_2007-07-28_2.jpg/220px-Iceberg_with_hole_near_Sandersons_Hope_2007-07-28_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Iceberg_with_hole_near_Sandersons_Hope_2007-07-28_2.jpg/330px-Iceberg_with_hole_near_Sandersons_Hope_2007-07-28_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Iceberg_with_hole_near_Sandersons_Hope_2007-07-28_2.jpg/440px-Iceberg_with_hole_near_Sandersons_Hope_2007-07-28_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iceberg_with_hole_near_Sandersons_Hope_2007-07-28_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Water in three states: liquid, solid (<a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>), and gas (invisible <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> in the air). <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">Clouds</a> are accumulations of water droplets, <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensed</a> from vapor-saturated air.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

Video demonstrating states of water present in domestic life.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Water</b> is a transparent and nearly colorless <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">chemical substance</a> that is the main constituent of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">streams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluids</a> of most living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> is <b>H<sub>2</sub>O</b>, meaning that its <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a> contains one <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> that are connected by <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>. Strictly speaking, water refers to the <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a> state of a substance that prevails at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_ambient_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard ambient temperature and pressure">standard ambient temperature and pressure</a>; but it often refers also to its <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a> state (<a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>) or its <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gaseous</a> state (<a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a> or <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>). It also occurs in nature as <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">ice packs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">icebergs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dew" title="Dew">dew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>, and atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a>.</p>
<p>Water covers 71% of the Earth's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> It is vital for all known forms of <a href="/wiki/Life#Range_of_tolerance" title="Life">life</a>. On Earth, 96.5% of the planet's crust water is found in seas and oceans, 1.7% in groundwater, 1.7% in glaciers and the ice caps of Antarctica and Greenland, a small fraction in other large water bodies, 0.001% in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">air</a> as <a href="/wiki/Vapor" title="Vapor">vapor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a> (formed of ice and liquid water suspended in air), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-b1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Only 2.5% of this water is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a>, and 98.8% of that water is in ice (excepting ice in clouds) and <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a>. Less than 0.3% of all freshwater is in rivers, lakes, and the atmosphere, and an even smaller amount of the Earth's freshwater (0.003%) is contained within biological bodies and manufactured products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-b1-2">[2]</a></sup> A greater quantity of water is found in the earth's interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crockett_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-crockett-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Water on Earth moves continually through the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a> (<a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>), <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a>, usually reaching the sea. Evaporation and transpiration contribute to the precipitation over land. Large amounts of water are also chemically combined or <a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorbed</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mineral_hydration" title="Mineral hydration">hydrated minerals</a>.</p>
<p>Safe <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a> is essential to humans and other lifeforms even though it provides no <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">calories</a> or <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a>. Access to safe drinking water has improved over the last decades in almost every part of the world, but approximately one billion people still lack access to safe water and over 2.5 billion lack access to adequate <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-5">[5]</a></sup> There is a clear correlation between access to safe water and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product_per_capita" title="Gross domestic product per capita">gross domestic product per capita</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> However, some observers have estimated that by 2025 more than half of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> will be facing water-based vulnerability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> A report, issued in November 2009, suggests that by 2030, in some developing regions of the world, water demand will exceed supply by 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Water plays an important role in the <a href="/wiki/World_economy" title="World economy">world economy</a>. Approximately 70% of the freshwater used by humans goes to <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baroni2007_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baroni2007-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">Fishing</a> in salt and fresh water bodies is a major source of food for many parts of the world. Much of long-distance trade of <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>) and manufactured products is transported by <a href="/wiki/Boat" title="Boat">boats</a> through seas, rivers, lakes, and <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a>. Large quantities of water, ice, and steam are used for <a href="/wiki/Cooling" title="Cooling">cooling</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heating" title="Heating">heating</a>, in industry and homes. Water is an excellent <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> for a wide variety of chemical substances; as such it is widely used in industrial processes, and in <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washing" title="Washing">washing</a>. Water is also central to many sports and other forms of entertainment, such as <a href="/wiki/Human_swimming" title="Human swimming">swimming</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleasure_boat" title="Pleasure boat">pleasure boating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boat_racing" title="Boat racing">boat racing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surfing" title="Surfing">surfing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sport_fishing" title="Sport fishing">sport fishing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diving" title="Diving">diving</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word "water" comes from "Old English wæter, from Proto-Germanic *watar (source also of Old Saxon watar, Old Frisian wetir, Dutch water, Old High German wazzar, German Wasser, Old Norse vatn, Gothic wato "water"), from PIE *wod-or, suffixed form of root *wed-... "water; wet."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_and_physical_properties">Chemical and physical properties</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">Properties of water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_(data_page)" title="Water (data page)">Water (data page)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water_model" title="Water model">Water model</a></div>
<p>Water (<span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polar</a> <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compound</a> that is at <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a> a <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">tasteless</a> and <a href="/wiki/Odor" title="Odor">odorless</a> liquid, nearly <a href="/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency" title="Transparency and translucency">colorless</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Color_of_water" title="Color of water">hint of blue</a>. This simplest <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chalcogenide" title="Hydrogen chalcogenide">hydrogen chalcogenide</a> is by far the most studied chemical compound and is described as the "universal solvent" for its ability to dissolve many substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> This allows it to be the "<a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> of life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> It is the only common substance to exist as a <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">solid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">gas</a> in normal terrestrial conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States">States</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SnowflakesWilsonBentley.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1437" data-file-width="1124" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/SnowflakesWilsonBentley.jpg/220px-SnowflakesWilsonBentley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/SnowflakesWilsonBentley.jpg/330px-SnowflakesWilsonBentley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/SnowflakesWilsonBentley.jpg/440px-SnowflakesWilsonBentley.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SnowflakesWilsonBentley.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Snowflake" title="Snowflake">Snowflakes</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Wilson_Bentley" title="Wilson Bentley">Wilson Bentley</a>, 1902.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brindis_(24675281395).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Brindis_%2824675281395%29.jpg/220px-Brindis_%2824675281395%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Brindis_%2824675281395%29.jpg/330px-Brindis_%2824675281395%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Brindis_%2824675281395%29.jpg/440px-Brindis_%2824675281395%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brindis_(24675281395).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Liquid water, showing droplets and air bubbles caused by the drops</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Water is a liquid at the temperatures and pressures that are most adequate for life. Specifically, at a standard pressure of 1 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atm</a> (1.01325 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a>, 101.325 k<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a>, 14.69595 <a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psi</a>), water is a liquid between the temperatures of 273.15 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> (0 <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a>, 32 <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">°F</a>) and 373.15 K (100 °C, 212 °F). Increasing the pressure slightly lowers the <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a>, which is about −5 °C at 600 atm, −22 °C at 2100 atm. This effect is relevant, for example, to <a href="/wiki/Ice_skating" title="Ice skating">ice skating</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Vostok" title="Lake Vostok">buried lakes of Antartica</a>, and to the movement of <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>. (At pressures higher than 2100 atm the melting point rapidly increases again, and ice takes several exotic forms that do not exist at lower pressures.)</p>
<p>Increasing the pressure has a more dramatic effect on the <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a>, that is about 374 °C at 220 atm. This effect is important in, among other things, deep-sea <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vents" title="Hydrothermal vents">hydrothermal vents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pressure_cooking" title="Pressure cooking">pressure cooking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a> design. At the top of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a>, where the atmospheric pressure is about 0.34 atm, water boils at 68 °C (154 °F).</p>
<p>At very low pressures (below about 0.006 atm), water cannot exist in the liquid state and passes directly from solid to gas by <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a>—a phenomenon exploited in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeze_drying" title="Freeze drying">freeze drying</a> of food. At very high pressures (above 221 atm), the liquid and gas states are no longer distinguishable, a state called <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical steam</a>.</p>
<p>Water also differs from most liquids in that it becomes less <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">dense</a> as it freezes. The maximum density of water in its liquid form (at 1atm) is 1,000 kg/m<sup>3</sup> (62.43 lb/cu ft); that occurs at 3.98 °C (39.16 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatureWaterStructure_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatureWaterStructure-15">[15]</a></sup> The density of ice is 917 kg/m<sup>3</sup> (57.25 lb/cu ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Thus, water expands 9% in volume as it freezes, which accounts for the fact that ice floats on liquid water.</p>
<p>The details of the exact chemical nature of liquid water are not well understood; some theories suggest that water's unusual behaviour is as a result of it having 2 liquid states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatureWaterStructure_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-NatureWaterStructure-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taste_and_odor">Taste and odor</span></h3>
<p>Pure water is usually described as tasteless and odorless, although humans have specific sensors that can feel the presence of water in their mouths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emo_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-emo-21">[21]</a></sup> and frogs are known to be able to smell it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frog_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-frog-22">[22]</a></sup> However, water from ordinary sources (including bottled mineral water) usually has many dissolved substances, that may give it varying tastes and odors. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">Humans</a> and other animals have developed senses that enable them to evaluate the <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">potability</a> of water by avoiding water that is too salty or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Putrid" title="Putrid">putrid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-candau_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-candau-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Color_and_appearance">Color and appearance</span></h3>
<p>The apparent color of natural bodies of water (and swimming pools) is often determined more by dissolved and suspended solids, or by reflection of the sky, than by water itself.</p>
<p>Light in the visible <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a> can traverse a couple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">meters</a> of pure water (or ice) without significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_absorption" title="Optical absorption">absorption</a>, so that it looks <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transparency_(optics)" title="Transparency (optics)">transparent</a> and colorless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Thus <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_plant" title="Aquatic plant">aquatic plants</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alga" title="Alga">algae</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> organisms can live in water up to hundreds of meters deep, because <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> can reach them. Water vapour is essentially invisible as a gas.</p>
<p>Through a thickness of 10 meters or more, however, the intrinsic <a href="/wiki/Color_of_water" title="Color of water">color of water</a> (or ice) is visibly <a href="/wiki/Turquoise_(color)" title="Turquoise (color)">turquoise</a> (greenish blue), as its <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_absorption_by_water" title="Electromagnetic absorption by water">absorption spectrum</a> has a sharp minimum at the corresponding color of light (1/227 m<sup>−1</sup> at 418 nm). The color becomes increasingly stronger and darker with increasing thickness. (Practically no sunlight reaches the parts of the oceans below 1000 meters of depth.) Infrared and ultraviolet light, on the other hand, is strongly <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">absorbed</a> by water.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refraction_index" title="Refraction index">refraction index</a> of liquid water (1.333 at 20 °C) is much higher than that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a> (1.0), similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, but lower than those of <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> (1.473), <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> (1.501), <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a> (1.627), and common types of <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> (1.4 to 1.6). The refraction index of ice (1.31) is lower than that of liquid water.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarity_and_hydrogen_bonding">Polarity and hydrogen bonding</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bonding_of_H2O" title="Chemical bonding of H2O">Chemical bonding of H2O</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="400" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg/220px-3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg/330px-3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg/440px-3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Model of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> (1) between molecules of water.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Capillarity.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="733" data-file-width="549" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Capillarity.svg/170px-Capillarity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Capillarity.svg/255px-Capillarity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Capillarity.svg/340px-Capillarity.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Capillarity.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Capillary_action" title="Capillary action">Capillary action</a> of water compared to <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_drop_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2475" data-file-width="3714" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Water_drop_001.jpg/220px-Water_drop_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Water_drop_001.jpg/330px-Water_drop_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Water_drop_001.jpg/440px-Water_drop_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_drop_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Impact from a water drop causes an upward "rebound" jet surrounded by circular <a href="/wiki/Capillary_wave" title="Capillary wave">capillary waves</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the water molecule is not linear and the oxygen atom has a higher <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> than hydrogen atoms, it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule">polar molecule</a>, with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_dipole_moment" title="Electrical dipole moment">electrical dipole moment</a>: the oxygen atom carries a slight negative charge, whereas the hydrogen atoms are slightly positive. Water is a good polar <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>, that dissolves many <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophilic" title="Hydrophilic">hydrophilic</a> organic molecules such as <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a> and simple <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>. Most <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a> dissolve in water to yield the corresponding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anions</a>. Many substances in living organisms, such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a>, are dissolved in water. Water also dissolves many <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gases</a>, such as oxygen and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>—the latter giving the fizz of <a href="/wiki/Carbonation" title="Carbonation">carbonated</a> beverages, <a href="/wiki/Sparkling_wine" title="Sparkling wine">sparkling wines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beers</a>.</p>
<p>On the other hand, many organic substances (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipids" title="Lipids">fats and oils</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkanes" title="Alkanes">alkanes</a>) are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a>, that is, insoluble in water. Many inorganic substances are insoluble too, including most metal <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>.</p>
<p>Because of its polarity, a molecule of water in the liquid or solid state can form up to four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bonds" title="Hydrogen bonds">hydrogen bonds</a> with neighboring molecules. These bonds are the cause of water's high <a href="/wiki/Surface_tension" title="Surface tension">surface tension</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and capillary forces. The <a href="/wiki/Capillary_action" title="Capillary action">capillary action</a> refers to the tendency of water to move up a narrow tube against the force of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. This property is relied upon by all <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a>, such as trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hydrogen bonds are also the reason why the melting and boiling points of water are much higher than those of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chalcogenide" title="Hydrogen chalcogenide">other analogous compounds</a> like <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span>S</span>). They also explain its exceptionally high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat capacity</a> (about 4.2 <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">J</a>/<a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">g</a>/K), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_of_fusion" title="Heat of fusion">heat of fusion</a> (about 333 J/g), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_of_vaporization" title="Heat of vaporization">heat of vaporization</a> (<span class="nowrap">2257 J/g</span>), and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> (between 0.561 and 0.679 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">W</a>/m/K). These properties make water more effective at moderating Earth's <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, by storing heat and transporting it between the oceans and the atmosphere.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_conductivity_and_electrolysis">Electrical conductivity and electrolysis</span></h3>
<p>Pure water has a low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>, which increases with the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_(chemistry)" title="Dissolution (chemistry)">dissolution</a> of a small amount of ionic material such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">common salt</a>.</p>
<p>Liquid water can be split into the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> hydrogen and oxygen by passing an electric current through it—a process called <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis</a>. The decomposition requires more <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> input than the <a href="/wiki/Standard_enthalpy_of_formation" title="Standard enthalpy of formation">heat released by the inverse process</a> (285.8 kJ/<a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mol</a>, or 15.9 MJ/kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_properties">Mechanical properties</span></h3>
<p>Liquid water can be assumed to be incompressible for most purposes: its compressibility ranges from 4.4 to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6990510000000000000♠</span>5.1<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−10</sup> Pa<sup>−1</sup></span> in ordinary conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Even in <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a> at 4 km depth, where the pressure is 400 atm, water suffers only a 1.8% decrease in volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nave_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-nave-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> of water is about 10<sup>−3</sup> Pa·<a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">s</a> or 0.01 <a href="/wiki/Poise" title="Poise">poise</a> at 20 °C, and the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a> in liquid water ranges between 1400 and 1540 m/s depending on temperature. Sound travels long distances in water with little attenuation, especially at low frequencies (roughly 0.03 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">dB</a>/km for 1 k<a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">Hz</a>), a property that is exploited by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetaceans" title="Cetaceans">cetaceans</a> and humans for communication and environment sensing (<a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPLcalc_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPLcalc-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactivity">Reactivity</span></h3>
<p>Elements which are more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electropositivity" title="Electropositivity">electropositive</a> than hydrogen such as <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> displace hydrogen from water, forming <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxides</a> and releasing hydrogen.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Earth">On Earth</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">Hydrology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_distribution_on_Earth" title="Water distribution on Earth">Water distribution on Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3002" data-file-width="3000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/220px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/330px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/440px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Water covers 71% of the Earth's surface; the oceans contain 96.5% of the Earth's water. The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a>, which contains 61% of all fresh water on Earth, is visible at the bottom. Condensed atmospheric water can be seen as <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a>, contributing to the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hydrology is the study of the movement, distribution, and quality of water throughout the Earth. The study of the distribution of water is <a href="/wiki/Hydrography" title="Hydrography">hydrography</a>. The study of the distribution and movement of groundwater is <a href="/wiki/Hydrogeology" title="Hydrogeology">hydrogeology</a>, of glaciers is <a href="/wiki/Glaciology" title="Glaciology">glaciology</a>, of inland waters is <a href="/wiki/Limnology" title="Limnology">limnology</a> and distribution of oceans is <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a>. Ecological processes with hydrology are in focus of <a href="/wiki/Ecohydrology" title="Ecohydrology">ecohydrology</a>.</p>
<p>The collective mass of water found on, under, and over the surface of a planet is called the <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>. Earth's approximate water volume (the total water supply of the world) is 1,338,000,000 km<sup>3</sup> (321,000,000 mi<sup>3</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-b1-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Liquid water is found in <a href="/wiki/Body_of_water" title="Body of water">bodies of water</a>, such as an ocean, <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lake</a>, <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">stream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pond" title="Pond">pond</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Puddle" title="Puddle">puddle</a>. The majority of water on Earth is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_water" title="Sea water">sea water</a>. Water is also present in the atmosphere in solid, liquid, and vapor states. It also exists as groundwater in <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>.</p>
<p>Water is important in many geological processes. Groundwater is present in most <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a>, and the pressure of this groundwater affects patterns of <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faulting</a>. Water in the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> is responsible for the melt that produces <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subduction_zone" title="Subduction zone">subduction zones</a>. On the surface of the Earth, water is important in both chemical and physical <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> processes. Water, and to a lesser but still significant extent, ice, are also responsible for a large amount of <a href="/wiki/Sediment_transport" title="Sediment transport">sediment transport</a> that occurs on the surface of the earth. <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">Deposition</a> of transported sediment forms many types of <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a>, which make up the <a href="/wiki/Geologic_record" title="Geologic record">geologic record</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Earth" title="History of the Earth">Earth history</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_cycle">Water cycle</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">Water cycle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_cycle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="589" data-file-width="860" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Water_cycle.png/220px-Water_cycle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Water_cycle.png/330px-Water_cycle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Water_cycle.png/440px-Water_cycle.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_cycle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">Water cycle</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> (known scientifically as the <b>hydrologic cycle</b>) refers to the continuous exchange of water within the <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>, between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_atmosphere" title="Earth atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> water, <a href="/wiki/Surface_water" title="Surface water">surface water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>.</p>
<p>Water moves perpetually through each of these regions in the <i>water cycle</i> consisting of following transfer processes:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> from oceans and other water bodies into the air and <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a> from land plants and animals into air.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>, from water vapor condensing from the air and falling to earth or ocean.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runoff_(water)" title="Runoff (water)">runoff</a> from the land usually reaching the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Most water vapor over the oceans returns to the oceans, but winds carry water vapor over land at the same rate as runoff into the sea, about 47 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_tonne_unit" title="Metric tonne unit">Tt</a> per year. Over land, evaporation and transpiration contribute another 72 Tt per year. Precipitation, at a rate of 119 Tt per year over land, has several forms: most commonly <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>, with some contribution from <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dew" title="Dew">dew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Dew is small drops of water that are condensed when a high density of water vapor meets a cool surface. Dew usually forms in the morning when the temperature is the lowest, just before sunrise and when the temperature of the earth's surface starts to increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Condensed water in the air may also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refract" title="Refract">refract</a> <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbows</a>.</p>
<p>Water runoff often collects over <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">watersheds</a> flowing into rivers. A mathematical model used to simulate river or stream flow and calculate water quality parameters is a <a href="/wiki/Hydrological_transport_model" title="Hydrological transport model">hydrological transport model</a>. Some water is diverted to <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> for agriculture. Rivers and seas offer opportunity for <a href="/wiki/Travel" title="Travel">travel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a>. Through <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>, runoff shapes the environment creating river <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valleys</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">deltas</a> which provide rich soil and level ground for the establishment of population centers. A <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">flood</a> occurs when an area of land, usually low-lying, is covered with water. It is when a river overflows its banks or flood comes from the sea. A <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a> is an extended period of months or years when a region notes a deficiency in its water supply. This occurs when a region receives consistently below average precipitation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fresh_water_storage">Fresh water storage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner noresize" style="width:402px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="width:400px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bay_of_Fundy_High_Tide.jpg"><img alt="Bay of Fundy High Tide.jpg" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="300" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bay_of_Fundy_High_Tide.jpg/200px-Bay_of_Fundy_High_Tide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Bay_of_Fundy_High_Tide.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bay_of_Fundy_Low_Tide.jpg"><img alt="Bay of Fundy Low Tide.jpg" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="300" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Bay_of_Fundy_Low_Tide.jpg/200px-Bay_of_Fundy_Low_Tide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/Bay_of_Fundy_Low_Tide.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Fundy" title="Bay of Fundy">Bay of Fundy</a> at high tide (left) and low tide (right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">Water resources</a></div>
<p>Some runoff water is trapped for periods of time, for example in lakes. At high altitude, during winter, and in the far north and south, snow collects in ice caps, snow pack and glaciers. Water also infiltrates the ground and goes into aquifers. This groundwater later flows back to the surface in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrosphere)" title="Spring (hydrosphere)">springs</a>, or more spectacularly in <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>. Groundwater is also extracted artificially in <a href="/wiki/Water_well" title="Water well">wells</a>. This water storage is important, since clean, fresh water is essential to human and other land-based life. In many parts of the world, it is in short supply.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_water_and_tides">Sea water and tides</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">Seawater</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tides" title="Tides">Tides</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">Sea water</a> contains about 3.5% <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> on average, plus smaller amounts of other substances. The physical properties of sea water differ from fresh water in some important respects. It freezes at a lower temperature (about −1.9 °C) and its density increases with decreasing temperature to the freezing point, instead of reaching maximum density at a temperature above freezing. The salinity of water in major seas varies from about 0.7% in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> to 4.0% in the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. (The <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a>, known for its ultra-high salinity levels of between 30–40%, is really a <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">salt lake</a>.)</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">Tides</a> are the cyclic rising and falling of local sea levels caused by the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal forces</a> of the Moon and the Sun acting on the oceans. Tides cause changes in the depth of the marine and <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuarine</a> water bodies and produce oscillating currents known as tidal streams. The changing tide produced at a given location is the result of the changing positions of the Moon and Sun relative to the Earth coupled with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">effects of Earth rotation</a> and the local <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a>. The strip of seashore that is submerged at high tide and exposed at low tide, the <a href="/wiki/Intertidal_zone" title="Intertidal zone">intertidal zone</a>, is an important ecological product of ocean tides.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_life">Effects on life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oasis_in_Libya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Oasis_in_Libya.jpg/220px-Oasis_in_Libya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Oasis_in_Libya.jpg/330px-Oasis_in_Libya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Oasis_in_Libya.jpg/440px-Oasis_in_Libya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oasis_in_Libya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">oasis</a> is an isolated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_source" title="Water source">water source</a> with <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">vegetation</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="1633" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg/220px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg/330px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg/440px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Overview of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis"><span style="color:green;">photosynthesis</span></a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration"><span style="color:red;">respiration</span></a>. Water (at right), together with carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>), form oxygen and organic compounds (at left), which can be respired to water and (CO<sub>2</sub>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From a <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological</a> standpoint, water has many distinct properties that are critical for the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>. It carries out this role by allowing <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> to react in ways that ultimately allow <a href="/wiki/Self-replication" title="Self-replication">replication</a>. All known forms of life depend on water. Water is vital both as a <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> in which many of the body's solutes dissolve and as an essential part of many <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic</a> processes within the body. Metabolism is the sum total of anabolism and catabolism. In anabolism, water is removed from molecules (through energy requiring enzymatic chemical reactions) in order to grow larger molecules (e.g. starches, triglycerides and proteins for storage of fuels and information). In catabolism, water is used to break bonds in order to generate smaller molecules (e.g. glucose, fatty acids and amino acids to be used for fuels for energy use or other purposes). Without water, these particular metabolic processes could not exist.</p>
<p>Water is fundamental to photosynthesis and respiration. Photosynthetic cells use the sun's energy to split off water's hydrogen from oxygen. Hydrogen is combined with CO<sub>2</sub> (absorbed from air or water) to form glucose and release oxygen. All living cells use such fuels and oxidize the hydrogen and carbon to capture the sun's energy and reform water and CO<sub>2</sub> in the process (cellular respiration).</p>
<p>Water is also central to acid-base neutrality and enzyme function. An acid, a hydrogen ion (H<sup>+</sup>, that is, a proton) donor, can be neutralized by a base, a proton acceptor such as a hydroxide ion (OH<sup>−</sup>) to form water. Water is considered to be neutral, with a <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> (the negative log of the hydrogen ion concentration) of 7. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acids" title="Acids">Acids</a> have pH values less than 7 while <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">bases</a> have values greater than 7.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aquatic_life_forms">Aquatic life forms</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hydrobiology" title="Hydrobiology">Hydrobiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_life" title="Marine life">Marine life</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_plant" title="Aquatic plant">Aquatic plant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/170px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/255px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/340px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Some of the <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reef</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="1796" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg/220px-Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg/330px-Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg/440px-Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Some marine <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">diatoms</a> – a key <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> group</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Earth surface waters are filled with life. The earliest life forms appeared in water; nearly all <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> live exclusively in water, and there are many types of marine mammals, such as <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whales</a>. Some kinds of animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, spend portions of their lives in water and portions on land. Plants such as <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> grow in the water and are the basis for some underwater ecosystems. <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">Plankton</a> is generally the foundation of the ocean <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>.</p>
<p>Aquatic vertebrates must obtain oxygen to survive, and they do so in various ways. Fish have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gills" title="Gills">gills</a> instead of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lungs" title="Lungs">lungs</a>, although some species of fish, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a>, have both. <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">Marine mammals</a>, such as dolphins, whales, <a href="/wiki/Otter" title="Otter">otters</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a> need to surface periodically to breathe air. Some amphibians are able to absorb oxygen through their skin. Invertebrates exhibit a wide range of modifications to survive in poorly oxygenated waters including breathing tubes (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siphon_(insect)" title="Siphon (insect)">insect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siphon_(mollusc)" title="Siphon (mollusc)">mollusc siphons</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gills" title="Gills">gills</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Carcinus" title="Carcinus">Carcinus</a></i>). However as invertebrate life evolved in an aquatic habitat most have little or no specialisation for respiration in water.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_human_civilization">Effects on human civilization</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg/220px-Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg/330px-Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg/440px-Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Water <a href="/wiki/Fountain" title="Fountain">fountain</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Civilization has historically flourished around rivers and major waterways; <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the so-called cradle of civilization, was situated between the major rivers <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>; the ancient society of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> depended entirely upon the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> was also founded on the banks of the Italian river <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a>. Large <a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">metropolises</a> like <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> owe their success in part to their easy accessibility via water and the resultant expansion of trade. Islands with safe water ports, like <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, have flourished for the same reason. In places such as <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, where water is more scarce, access to clean drinking water was and is a major factor in human development.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_pollution">Health and pollution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg/220px-Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg/330px-Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg/440px-Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An environmental science program – a student from <a href="/wiki/Iowa_State_University" title="Iowa State University">Iowa State University</a> sampling water</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Water fit for human consumption is called drinking water or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potable_water" title="Potable water">potable water</a>. Water that is not potable may be made potable by filtration or <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a>, or by a range of <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">other methods</a>.</p>
<p>Water that is not fit for drinking but is not harmful for humans when used for swimming or bathing is called by various names other than potable or drinking water, and is sometimes called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safe_water" title="Safe water">safe water</a>, or "safe for bathing". Chlorine is a skin and mucous membrane irritant that is used to make water safe for bathing or drinking. Its use is highly technical and is usually monitored by government regulations (typically 1 part per million (ppm) for drinking water, and 1–2 ppm of chlorine not yet reacted with impurities for bathing water). Water for bathing may be maintained in satisfactory microbiological condition using chemical disinfectants such as <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> or by the use of <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light.</p>
<p>In the USA, non-potable forms of <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> generated by humans may be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Greywater" title="Greywater">greywater</a>, which is treatable and thus easily able to be made potable again, and <a href="/wiki/Blackwater_(waste)" title="Blackwater (waste)">blackwater</a>, which generally contains <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> and other forms of waste which require <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">further treatment</a> in order to be made reusable. Greywater composes 50–80% of residential wastewater generated by a household's sanitation equipment (<a href="/wiki/Sink" title="Sink">sinks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shower" title="Shower">showers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kitchen" title="Kitchen">kitchen</a> runoff, but not <a href="/wiki/Toilet" title="Toilet">toilets</a>, which generate blackwater.) These terms may have different meanings in other countries and cultures.</p>
<p>This natural resource is becoming scarcer in certain places, and its availability is a major social and economic concern. Currently, about a billion people around the world routinely drink unhealthy water. Most countries accepted the goal of halving by 2015 the number of people worldwide who do not have access to safe water and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> during the <a href="/wiki/29th_G8_summit" title="29th G8 summit">2003 G8 Evian summit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Even if this difficult goal is met, it will still leave more than an estimated half a billion people without access to safe drinking water and over a billion without access to adequate sanitation. Poor <a href="/wiki/Water_quality" title="Water quality">water quality</a> and bad sanitation are deadly; some five million deaths a year are caused by polluted drinking water. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> estimates that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safe_water" title="Safe water">safe water</a> could prevent 1.4 million child deaths from <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a> each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Water, however, is not a finite resource (meaning the availability of water is limited), but rather re-circulated as potable water in precipitation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> in quantities many orders of magnitude higher than human consumption. Therefore, it is the relatively small quantity of water in reserve in the earth (about 1% of our drinking <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, which is replenished in aquifers around every 1 to 10 years)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, that is a <a href="/wiki/Non-renewable_resource" title="Non-renewable resource">non-renewable resource</a>, and it is, rather, the distribution of potable and irrigation water which is scarce<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="distribution can not be scarce, can be lacking, or inefficient, or unjust, or unfair (January 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, rather than the actual amount of it that exists on the earth. Water-poor countries use importation of goods as the primary method of importing water (to leave enough for local human consumption)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="water importation, via products? unclear for regular reader (January 2017)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, since the manufacturing process<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="which manufacturing process? (January 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> uses around 10 to 100 times products' masses in water<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="this whole paragraph needs amendment (January 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</p>
<p>In the developing world, 90% of all <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> still goes untreated into local rivers and streams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Some 50 countries, with roughly a third of the world's population, also suffer from medium or high water stress, and 17 of these extract more water annually than is recharged through their natural water cycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The strain not only affects surface freshwater bodies like rivers and lakes, but it also degrades groundwater resources.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_uses">Human uses</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">Water supply</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_2" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:640px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_3" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Subsurface_drip_emission_on_loamy_soil.ogv/640px--Subsurface_drip_emission_on_loamy_soil.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:640px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="46.52" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Subsurface_drip_emission_on_loamy_soil.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b1/Subsurface_drip_emission_on_loamy_soil.ogv/Subsurface_drip_emission_on_loamy_soil.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm;
